DELETION TEST (corrected to the narrowed scope): a small flag-derivation helper concentrates the github-enabled predicate that REAPPEARS in 4 sites and the open-signup coercion that REAPPEARS with 2 incompatible rules. That slice passes — complexity is smeared today and would concentrate. The backdating slice does not pass (already concentrated). So deletionTestPasses=true only for the narrowed finding.

SEVERITY: adjusted medium->low. Both flags are static at process start, the page-loader sites all use the same typed-env style among themselves, and the only cross-style mismatch (auth.ts process.env vs typed env) is forced by a real config-time constraint. The practical bug window is "someone edits one of the 4 github predicates and forgets the others" — annoyance-grade, not correctness-critical. No ADR conflict (decision list says nothing about env-reader topology; CLAUDE.md's import { env } from "void/env" mandate is consistent with consolidating, not opposed).

#79 · Cross-cutting concerns
Theme contract (`'theme'` key, dark default, class toggle) is encoded independently in the FOUC script and the toggle component
low severity effort: small confidence: high missing locality
Files
  • middleware/01.head.ts:25-33
  • src/components/theme-toggle.tsx:42-55
Problem — the friction

The FOUC-killer inline script in 01.head.ts hard-codes the localStorage key "theme", the values "dark"/"light", the dark-by-default rule, and the classList.toggle("dark", …) mechanism as a hand-written string. theme-toggle.tsx independently re-encodes the same four facts in TypeScript (reads/writes localStorage.setItem("theme", …), toggles the same .dark class). Solution — the deepened module

Extract a tiny theme-contract module (e.g. src/lib/theme.ts) exporting THEME_STORAGE_KEY, DARK_CLASS, DEFAULT_DARK plus readPreferredTheme()/applyTheme(isDark) helpers. theme-toggle.tsx imports them directly. For the FOUC script in 01.head.ts, build the innerHTML string by interpolating the same constants (a template literal — the inline script can't import at runtime), so the boot script and runtime toggle derive the key/class/default from one place. Note that ".dark" is also a CSS literal in styles.css (@custom-variant + .dark {}); that occurrence cannot be deduped by a JS constant, so document that the class name lives in two layers (JS constant + CSS) by design and keep them in lockstep.

Benefits — leverage & locality

Leverage: the toggle and any future theme-aware code read one source for the storage key and class name. Locality: 'rename the theme key' or 'default to light' is a one-constant change instead of editing a stringified script and a component that must stay byte-compatible.

Deletion test

Deleting the shared constants makes the duplication REAPPEAR across 2 sites (the head FOUC script and theme-toggle) that are correctness-coupled. Earns its keep as a small deepening that concentrates a currently-smeared invariant.

Testability impact

Makes readPreferredTheme()/applyTheme() unit-testable in isolation; today the FOUC behaviour is an untestable inline string and the toggle's logic is entangled with DOM/transition-suppression side effects.

Interface sketch
// src/lib/theme.ts export const THEME_STORAGE_KEY = 'theme'; export const DARK_CLASS = 'dark'; export function preferredIsDark(): boolean; // localStorage ?? default-dark export function applyTheme(dark: boolean): void; // toggles DARK_CLASS + persists
Verifier notes

Confirmed in both cited files. middleware/01.head.ts:28-30 hand-builds the FOUC script as a string literal encoding: storage key "theme", value test t==="dark", dark-by-default (?...:true), and classList.toggle("dark",d) — plus htmlAttrs:{class:"dark"} on line 24. theme-toggle.tsx independently re-encodes the same facts in TS: classList.contains("dark") (22,26), classList.toggle("dark",next) (42), localStorage.setItem("theme", next?"dark":"light") (52), and the ?? true dark-default (62). A repo-wide grep for the "theme" key + .dark JS usages returns ONLY these two files — no existing shared theme module, so the duplication is real, not a misread. The no-FOUC contract genuinely requires both sites to agree on {key, class, default}; nothing enforces it, and the head-comment even documents the coupling in prose, which is exactly the smell.

Deletion test passes: deleting a shared constants module makes the invariant reappear across the 2 correctness-coupled sites. theme-toggle is plain TS and can import the constants directly. CAVEAT the finder understates: the FOUC script ships as an innerHTML STRING in <head> and cannot import a TS module at runtime — the constants can only be interpolated into the script string at SSR/build time (a template literal referencing the constants). The solution's "generated from the same constants (or at minimum references the exported key)" wording already anticipates this, so it's achievable, but the script side is interpolation, not import-dedup. Worth being explicit so the implementer doesn't try to import inside the inline script.

Third coupling worth noting: the ".dark" class name is ALSO encoded as a CSS literal in src/styles.css (line 5 @custom-variant dark (&:is(.dark *)), lines 338/446 .dark {}). No JS constant can dedup the CSS occurrence, so a DARK_CLASS constant cannot be a true single source of truth for the class name — it concentrates the JS side only. This caps the leverage and confirms severity=low is correct (small interface, ~3 constants, one of which only partially dedups). No durable-decision conflict; adrConflict correctly (none). This is a legitimate small deepening that concentrates a currently-smeared, correctness-coupled invariant — keep at low.

Gap · Cron watchdog · 3 findings

#80 · Gap · Cron watchdog
Watchdog measures 'old since open', not 'stuck' — no liveness signal on the runs row
high severity effort: medium confidence: high deep but fragile touches a durable decision
Files
  • apps/dashboard/crons/sweep-stuck-runs.ts:25-30
  • apps/dashboard/db/schema.ts:213-214
  • apps/dashboard/src/lib/ingest.ts:654-702
  • apps/dashboard/env.ts:42-47
Problem — the friction

The cron's interface promises to find runs that are 'stuck' (CI SIGKILL'd, never called /complete), but the predicate is and(eq(runs.status, 'running'), lt(runs.createdAt, cutoff)) — it keys entirely off run-open time. The runs table (schema.ts:213-214) carries only createdAt + completedAt; there is no updatedAt/lastActivityAt that appendRunResults bumps on every /results flush (ingest.ts:690 bumps teams.lastActivityAt, never the run). Consequence: a legitimately long suite that is actively streaming results — exactly a suite still calling appendRunResults every few seconds — is indistinguishable from a process that died at onBegin. Once it crosses WRIGHTFUL_RUN_STALE_MINUTES (default 30) of wall-clock since open, the watchdog force-flips it to 'interrupted' and broadcasts a terminal event even though the reporter is still alive and will keep POSTing. The maintainer/AI reading this has to cross-reference three files to discover that the only knob (env.ts:42-47, raise the minutes) trades false-positive interrupts against slow detection of real deaths, with no way to win both. The env doc even claims the default is 'longer than any realistic single test run' — an assumption baked into a magic number instead of into a measurable signal.

Solution — the deepened module

Add a lastActivityAt integer column to runs, initialized to createdAt in openRun's insert (ingest.ts:587-613) so onBegin-only dead runs are still sweepable, and bumped to nowSeconds in the same existing D1 batch as every subsequent ingest write — alongside the aggregate-delta statement in appendRunResults (ingest.ts:678-689) and the status/recompute writes in completeRun/finalizeStaleRun — so there is no extra round-trip. Replace the cron predicate with a single named staleRunFilter(cutoff) returning and(eq(runs.status, "running"), lt(runs.lastActivityAt, cutoff)) ("no write activity for N minutes" = what 'stuck' actually means), exported so a future admin force-complete reuses the identical definition. Fold the column into the not-yet-deployed initial migration (durable decision #8). Note the secondary effect this also fixes: today a watchdog-interrupted-but-actually-passing run is permanently mislabeled because completeRun's severity merge (interrupted=3 > passed=1) rejects the reporter's late /complete — a correct liveness signal eliminates the false interrupt at the source.

Benefits — leverage & locality

LEVERAGE: callers (the cron, plus any future UI 'is this run actually dead?' indicator) get a truthful liveness signal instead of a proxy that conflates duration with death; the stale window can be tightened to minutes without false-interrupting long live suites. LOCALITY: the meaning of 'stuck' is concentrated in one column + one filter expression rather than smeared across the cron's WHERE clause, the env comment's prose assumptions, and the implicit knowledge that createdAt is the only timestamp available.

Deletion test

Deleting lastActivityAt + the staleRunFilter and reverting to lt(createdAt) makes the false-positive complexity REAPPEAR: every caller that wants to know if a run is dead (the cron today, a future admin force-complete, a future dashboard 'stalled?' badge) would each have to re-derive 'dead' from createdAt + a guessed window and each would mis-fire on long live suites. It earns its keep — N≥2 current/near-term callers of 'is this run stuck'.

Testability impact

Today 'is this run stuck' is untestable as a unit — it's an inline WHERE clause whose correctness depends on the absence of activity tracking that doesn't exist. A named staleRunFilter(cutoff) over a real lastActivityAt column is a pure predicate testable in isolation: insert a run with lastActivityAt just inside/outside the cutoff and assert membership, with no need to simulate a 30-minute-old createdAt or a full ingest integration.

Interface sketch
// schema: runs.lastActivityAt: integer (bumped in openRun + appendRunResults batch) function staleRunFilter(cutoffSeconds: number): SQL // and(eq(status,'running'), lt(lastActivityAt, cutoff)) // invariant: lastActivityAt monotonically advances on every ingest write to the run
Durable-decision note

Touches durable decision #8 (Drizzle migrations applied on deploy / initial migration may be regenerated since nothing has deployed). Adding lastActivityAt is a schema change — fine to fold into the not-yet-deployed initial migration. Worth it because the current design force-interrupts live runs, a correctness bug the watchdog's own docstring contradicts.

Verifier notes

Every cited line verified.

- sweep-stuck-runs.ts:30 — predicate is exactly and(eq(runs.status, "running"), lt(runs.createdAt, cutoff)). The docstring (lines 8-19) explicitly frames this as finding runs that are "stuck" (CI SIGKILL'd, never called /complete), so the interface promise vs. the createdAt-only mechanism is a genuine mismatch.
- schema.ts:213-214 — runs carries only createdAt + completedAt. No updatedAt/lastActivityAt on runs. lastActivityAt exists only on teams (schema.ts:39). The updatedAt hits at 124/142 are on other tables, not runs (runs block ends at 214). Confirmed.
- ingest.ts:690 — appendRunResults calls bumpTeamActivity(scope.teamId, ...) which updates teams.lastActivityAt (bumpTeamActivity body at 468-476), never the run row. The per-/results batch (678-689) updates only the aggregate counters on runs, not any timestamp. So a live, actively-streaming long suite is genuinely indistinguishable from a process that died at onBegin — both have a fixed createdAt and an unchanging run row w.r.t. time.
- env.ts:42-47 — default 30 min, doc literally says "longer than any realistic single test run", i.e. an assumption baked into a magic number. Confirmed.

The friction is real AND the blast radius is WORSE than the finding states. I traced completeRun's merge: once the watchdog flips a still-alive run to interrupted, the run is no longer 'running', so the late /complete from the live reporter no longer takes the verbatim branch (statusExpr line 783) — it hits the severity branch. interrupted = severity 3 (RUN_STATUS_SEVERITY line 720), while passed=1 and flaky=2. So a slow-but-passing suite that the watchdog killed is PERMANENTLY recorded as interrupted — the reporter's own passed /complete is rejected as a downgrade. Only failed/timedout (sev 4) could overwrite. That's silent data corruption on a legitimate run, not merely a transient mislabel.

Deletion test: PASSES, but with an honest correction to the finder's "N≥2 current callers" claim. I grepped — lt(runs.createdAt, cutoff) / staleness derivation appears in EXACTLY ONE place today (the cron). The "admin force-complete" and "stalled? badge" callers are genuinely hypothetical/near-term, not current. So this is closer to a one-current-caller situation. HOWEVER, the deepening still earns its keep on a different axis than caller-count: it does not just move complexity, it CORRECTS a false-positive bug by replacing a wrong signal (open-time) with the right one (last-write-time). The named staleRunFilter(cutoff) predicate also concentrates the "definition of stuck" so the next caller can't re-derive it wrong. That is concentration-of-correctness, which the deletion test rewards: revert it and the bug reappears the moment anyone else asks "is this run dead?".

Solution is sound and low-cost: a lastActivityAt integer on runs, bumped in the SAME existing batch as openRun's insert and appendRunResults' aggregate statement (lines 615-624, 678-689) — no extra round-trip, and completeRun/finalizeStaleRun already write the run row so they'd set it too. No ADR conflict: per durable decision #8 the initial migration is not yet deployed and can be regenerated in place, so adding the column is free. Decisions #1/#2/#10 untouched (single D1, logical tenancy, still one batch ≤99 params).

One sharpening for the solution: the predicate must use coalesce(lastActivityAt, createdAt) (or backfill lastActivityAt on insert) so runs that opened but never streamed a single result still have a comparable timestamp — otherwise a NULL lastActivityAt on a truly-dead onBegin-only run would never satisfy lt(...) and would never be swept, regressing the original detection. Setting lastActivityAt=createdAt in openRun's insert (as I note above) handles this cleanly.

#81 · Gap · Cron watchdog
Watchdog cron can self-DoS on a mass-stranding event: unbounded sweep SELECT + strict-serial finalize, no per-pass budget
medium severity effort: medium confidence: high deep but fragile revised by verifier
Files
  • apps/dashboard/crons/sweep-stuck-runs.ts:27-48
  • apps/dashboard/src/lib/ingest.ts:824-849
Problem — the friction

The sweep SELECT (sweep-stuck-runs.ts:27-30) has no LIMIT, and the loop (lines 33-44) awaits finalizeStaleRun one run at a time. Each finalizeStaleRun (ingest.ts:828-848) issues a db.batch (an UPDATE + a 5-subquery aggregate recompute) AND an awaited broadcastRunUpdate (a void/live publish RPC) — so every stale run is at minimum two serial round-trips. The failure mode this watchdog exists to handle is precisely a mass-stranding event: an ingest outage or a CI provider hiccup leaves thousands of runs at status='running'. When that happens, the cron picks up the entire backlog in one query and tries to drain it serially inside a single scheduled invocation. That blows the Worker subrequest cap and/or CPU/wall-time budget long before it finishes, the invocation is killed mid-drain, and (because there's no progress checkpoint and no per-run lock) the NEXT invocation re-selects the same enormous set and dies the same way — the watchdog can permanently fail to drain exactly when it's needed most. There is also no cap on broadcast fan-out: thousands of publishRunUpdate RPCs serially.

Solution — the deepened module

Make the watchdog drain a bounded slice per invocation: add .limit(batchSize) to the sweep SELECT and replace the strict serial await with bounded-concurrency finalization (e.g. Promise.allSettled over small chunks — a chunkByParams-style helper already exists at ingest.ts:58 to model the chunking shape). Each 5-minute invocation then makes guaranteed forward progress on a capped number of runs and stays well under the Workers subrequest/CPU budget; the backlog drains incrementally across successive invocations (each pass naturally re-scans only still-'running' rows). Optionally add an index on runs.status (or composite status+createdAt) so the sweep SELECT isn't a scan. Justify the change as a robustness fix for the watchdog's own failure mode, NOT as concentrating multi-caller complexity — there is currently one caller. A sweepStaleRuns({ cutoff, limit, now }) seam is reasonable to host the limit/concurrency/counting policy, but present it as 'one tidy home for the budget policy', not as an N>=2 deletion-test win.

Benefits — leverage & locality

LEVERAGE: the cron caller stops carrying drain-strategy knowledge; it just invokes a bounded sweep that is guaranteed to fit the Worker budget and always make progress. Backlog drains predictably across passes instead of starving. LOCALITY: subrequest/CPU-budget reasoning is concentrated in one tunable module (limit + concurrency in one place) instead of being an emergent property of an unbounded SELECT plus a serial for-loop spread across the cron file.

Deletion test

Deleting the bounded sweep module and inlining the loop back into the cron makes the budget-management complexity REAPPEAR in the cron body, and any second trigger of finalization (a manual admin 'reap stuck runs' endpoint, or a backfill script) would each re-implement LIMIT + chunked concurrency + partial-progress counting. N≥2 plausible callers; it concentrates real, currently-absent complexity, so it earns its keep.

Testability impact

Today the drain behavior is only observable by running the actual cron against a populated D1 — untestable as a unit, and the catastrophic case (thousands of rows) is exactly what no test exercises. A sweepStaleRuns(opts) function with injectable cutoff/limit is unit-testable: assert it selects at most limit, that a thrown finalize on one run doesn't abort the batch (allSettled), and that the returned {found, finalized, remaining} counts are correct — none of which is reachable through the current inline loop.

Interface sketch
async function sweepStaleRuns(opts: { cutoffSeconds: number; limit: number; now: number }): Promise<{ found: number; finalized: number; failed: number }> // invariant: selects <= limit running rows per pass; bounded concurrency; partial failure never aborts the pass
Verifier notes

All technical claims verified against the cited files. sweep-stuck-runs.ts:27-30 has NO LIMIT on the SELECT (filters status='running' AND createdAt<cutoff). There is also no index on runs.status (db/schema.ts only has runs_project_created_at_idx and friends — all keyed on projectId first), so the sweep is effectively a status-filtered scan, compounding cost. The loop (lines 33-44) is strictly serial await finalizeStaleRun. finalizeStaleRun (ingest.ts:824-848) issues db.batch([UPDATE, aggregateRecomputeStatement(...)]) then an awaited broadcastRunUpdate. aggregateRecomputeStatement (ingest.ts:429-444) is ONE UPDATE statement carrying 5 correlated COUNT subqueries — so the finder's "5-subquery aggregate recompute" is accurate in substance (5 subqueries), though it's one statement, not five. broadcastRunUpdate (ingest.ts:503-513) awaits publishRunUpdate (a void/live RPC). So ~2 serial subrequests per stale run is correct. On a mass-stranding event (the exact scenario the watchdog exists for) this drains an unbounded backlog serially in one scheduled invocation → genuine Cloudflare subrequest-cap / CPU / wall-time blowup. The fragility is real and is a legitimate robustness defect.

Two corrections to the finder's framing: (1) DELETION TEST FAILS as framed. The finder claims N>=2 callers (a manual admin 'reap stuck runs' endpoint and a backfill script). I grepped the whole tree: finalizeStaleRun has exactly ONE caller (the cron), and no reap/backfill endpoint exists. Both extra callers are speculative/future, not present. So extracting sweepStaleRuns({cutoff,limit,now}) is a one-adapter seam today; it MOVES the not-yet-written budget logic into a module with a single caller rather than concentrating complexity smeared across N existing callers. The recommendation's merit is a correctness fix (a watchdog that can self-DoS under exactly its design load), which happens to have a clean seam — not a smeared-complexity deepening. (2) The finder slightly overstates "permanently fail to drain": the UPDATE is guarded by eq(runs.status,'running'), so runs it does finalize flip to 'interrupted' and drop out of the next SELECT — it grinds forward slowly while re-scanning the full set each pass, rather than making literally zero progress. Severity adjusted high->medium: the system is not yet deployed, the fix is small, and at the stated single-digit writes/sec/team the steady-state backlog is modest; the high-impact case requires a multi-tenant ingest outage. No ADR conflict — adding a LIMIT + bounded concurrency to the watchdog touches no durable decision.

#82 · Gap · Cron watchdog
Stale-run watchdog runs a redundant recompute + duplicate live broadcast on a no-op finalize (cron overlap / racing /complete)
low severity effort: small confidence: high deep but fragile revised by verifier
Files
  • apps/dashboard/crons/sweep-stuck-runs.ts:33-44
  • apps/dashboard/src/lib/ingest.ts:824-849
  • apps/dashboard/src/lib/ingest.ts:764-808
Problem — the friction

There is no lock or in-flight marker around finalization. Two finalize paths can target the same run concurrently: (a) two overlapping cron invocations if one pass runs long (the unbounded-loop problem above makes overlap likely), and (b) a real /complete (completeRun, ingest.ts:764-808) arriving from a finally-resurrected CI job at the same moment the sweep runs. The status='running' guard on finalizeStaleRun's UPDATE (ingest.ts:832-837) does make the *status flip* safe-ish — whichever write commits second matches zero rows and can't downgrade a run already moved off 'running'. BUT the aggregateRecomputeStatement in the same batch (ingest.ts:839) is NOT guarded on status: it recomputes counts from testResults via COUNT subqueries and runs regardless of whether the status UPDATE matched. So a double-finalize re-runs the full recompute + an awaited broadcastRunUpdate (ingest.ts:846-848) redundantly, and a finalize racing a /complete can broadcast a 'terminal interrupted' summary that contradicts the 'passed/failed' terminal that /complete just broadcast — last-writer-wins on the live socket, with the watchdog potentially clobbering a legitimately-completed run's terminal event in viewers' eyes even though the DB status itself is protected. The recompute is idempotent in the DB (COUNT from the same rows yields the same numbers), so there's no data corruption — but the wasted batch + the conflicting broadcast are real, and the safety relies on a subtle interaction between the guarded UPDATE and the unguarded recompute that nothing documents or tests.

Solution — the deepened module

Reframe as a low-severity efficiency nit, not a correctness/race fix: on a no-op finalize, skip the redundant recompute + broadcast. Read the rowsAffected (changes) from the guarded status UPDATE result (it is the first element of batchResults in finalizeStaleRun) and only run the broadcast when it reports 1 row changed; when 0, return early so cron overlap / a racing /complete don't emit a duplicate 'progress' event. Optionally fold the count recompute into the same status-guarded UPDATE so it cannot run for a no-op. Do NOT justify this as preventing a contradicting/clobbered terminal — the broadcast already carries the row's true status (AGGREGATE_SUMMARY_COLUMNS.status = runs.status), so no contradiction is possible. The only win is suppressing a wasted batch + a duplicate live event.

Benefits — leverage & locality

LEVERAGE: callers (the cron, and completeRun racing it) get a finalize that is genuinely once-only at the broadcast level, not just at the status-column level — no contradictory terminal events on the live socket, no redundant recompute under contention. LOCALITY: the 'who wins when two terminations race' invariant lives entirely inside finalize/complete rather than being an emergent, undocumented consequence of which statements happen to carry the status guard.

Deletion test

This is tightening an existing module, not adding a pass-through, so the test is whether the guard concentrates real complexity: removing the conditional-broadcast logic makes the race-handling reappear at both callers (cron and completeRun would each need to reason about whether their broadcast might contradict the other's). It concentrates the cross-path termination ordering in one seam — earns its keep.

Testability impact

Currently the double-finalize / finalize-vs-complete race is only reasoned about by hand and never tested. Returning the guarded UPDATE's rowsAffected from finalizeStaleRun (or a {flipped: boolean} result) makes the once-only property unit-testable: call finalize twice on the same run and assert the second returns flipped=false and issues no broadcast; interleave a completeRun and assert exactly one terminal broadcast wins deterministically.

Interface sketch
async function finalizeStaleRun(run: {id;projectId;teamId}, completedAt: number): Promise<{ flipped: boolean }> // invariant: recompute + broadcastRunUpdate happen ONLY when the status='running'-guarded UPDATE flipped exactly one row; a no-op finalize is fully silent
Verifier notes

Read all cited code. The structural facts are correct but the load-bearing harm claim is FALSE.

CONFIRMED FACTS:
- finalizeStaleRun (ingest.ts:824-849): batch = [guarded status UPDATE, aggregateRecomputeStatement]. The status UPDATE (831-837) IS guarded on eq(runs.status,"running"); the recompute (839) and broadcast (846-848) run unconditionally, not gated on rowsAffected. No lock/idempotency marker. True.
- completeRun (ingest.ts:764-808) is the other finalize path; status merge is atomic-in-SQL (monotonic severity merge, 783) and also unconditionally recomputes + broadcasts.

THE CENTRAL CLAIM IS WRONG. The finder says a racing finalize can "broadcast a 'terminal interrupted' summary that contradicts the 'passed/failed' terminal" and "clobber a legitimately-completed run's terminal event in viewers' eyes." That does not happen. The broadcast summary is summaryRows[0] from aggregateRecomputeStatement's .returning(AGGREGATE_SUMMARY_COLUMNS) (444), and AGGREGATE_SUMMARY_COLUMNS.status = runs.status (ingest.ts:32) — i.e. the ACTUAL committed row status read back after the guarded UPDATE in the same atomic D1 batch. The recompute UPDATE only sets count columns (totalTests/passed/failed/flaky/skipped, 437-441); it never writes status. The only place "interrupted" is written is the guarded UPDATE (831), which no-ops when the run already left 'running'. So in the race (completeRun flips to 'passed' first), finalizeStaleRun's guarded UPDATE matches 0 rows, the recompute's .returning() reads back status:'passed', and the broadcast carries status:'passed' — the CORRECT terminal, not a contradicting 'interrupted'. The "watchdog clobbers a completed run's terminal in viewers' eyes" harm — the entire severity driver — does not exist.

RESIDUAL REAL ISSUE (minor): on a no-op finalize (guarded UPDATE matches 0 rows from cron overlap or a racing /complete), the recompute + broadcastRunUpdate still execute. The recompute is idempotent (COUNT from same rows), the broadcast is status-correct, so this is a wasted batch + a duplicate-but-consistent live 'progress' event. That is inefficiency, not corruption and not a contradicting event.

DELETION TEST FAILS AS FRAMED. The finder justifies depth by "race-handling reappears at both callers (cron and completeRun would each need to reason about whether their broadcast might contradict the other's)." That premise is false — the broadcasts can't contradict because status is read from the row, not synthesized. There is no complexity smeared across N callers: there are exactly two finalize functions and the cron has a single caller. The proposed gate would only suppress a redundant-but-correct broadcast; it concentrates almost nothing.

No ADR conflict. The guarded-UPDATE-in-batch design is consistent with decision 10 (db.batch atomic multi-statement writes) and the existing monotonic-severity-merge approach in completeRun.

Gap · Capability credentials · 2 findings

#83 · Gap · Capability credentials
No change.
medium severity effort: small confidence: high missing locality
Files
  • apps/dashboard/routes/api/teams/[teamSlug]/p/[projectSlug]/keys.ts:9
  • apps/dashboard/routes/api/teams/[teamSlug]/p/[projectSlug]/keys.ts:17
  • apps/dashboard/routes/api/teams/[teamSlug]/p/[projectSlug]/keys.ts:66
  • apps/dashboard/src/lib/api-key.ts:5
  • apps/dashboard/src/lib/api-key.ts:41
Problem — the friction

The API-key lifecycle has two halves that MUST agree on the exact byte representation — minting (keys.ts: generateApiKey + sha256Hex, then keyPrefix = token.slice(0,8) and keyHash = await sha256Hex(token)) and validation (api-key.ts: validateApiKey re-derives prefix = rawKey.slice(0,8) and hash via hashKey, then timingSafeEqualHex). But the mint half is hand-written inside a POST route handler with its own private sha256Hex copy, while the validate half is in src/lib. The two digest implementations are textually identical today, but nothing structurally couples them: if hashKey ever changed (e.g. switched to a salted or differently-encoded digest) the route's sha256Hex would silently diverge and every key minted after the change would fail validation — a self-inflicted auth outage that no test would catch because keys.ts has no test and the route owns its own crypto. The 8-char prefix slicing convention (load-bearing for the candidate lookup where(eq(apiKeys.keyPrefix, prefix))) is also duplicated as a bare .slice(0,8) in both files with no shared constant.

Solution — the deepened module

No change. Add mintApiKey to src/lib/api-key.ts so one module owns both produce and verify, sharing one digest function and one PREFIX_LEN constant; the keys POST route shrinks to authz plus label validation plus insert.

Benefits — leverage & locality

Leverage: the route handler no longer carries crypto; callers that mint keys get a triple whose prefix/hash are guaranteed to be the exact representation the validator looks up. Locality: the produce/verify pair for API keys lives in one file, so a change to the hashing or prefix scheme is a single edit that cannot desynchronize mint from validate — the class of 'every new key silently fails auth' bug is structurally removed.

Deletion test

Earning its keep as a concentration. The mint logic exists today only in keys.ts; co-locating it with the validator doesn't add a pass-through layer — it pulls scattered, must-agree logic into the one module that already owns the agreement contract. Callers: the keys POST route (mint) and validateApiKey / requireApiKeyOrResponse on every ingest request (verify). Two halves of one invariant, currently in two files.

Testability impact

A mintApiKey/validateApiKey pair in one module becomes round-trippable in a unit test: mint a key, assert validateApiKey accepts it and rejects a one-char-mutated variant, all without a route or HTTP context. Today the mint path (keys.ts) has zero coverage and can only be exercised through a full authenticated POST integration test that the project does not yet have (real-DB harness is known-outstanding), so the produce-verify agreement is currently untested by construction.

Interface sketch
// src/lib/api-key.ts export const KEY_PREFIX_LEN = 8; export async function mintApiKey(): Promise<{ token: string; keyPrefix: string; keyHash: string }>; // token = `wrf_` + base64url(24 rand) // validateApiKey unchanged; both halves now share one sha256Hex + KEY_PREFIX_LEN
Verifier notes

Verified both files. keys.ts:9-24 has private sha256Hex and generateApiKey; POST handler derives keyPrefix at line 69 and keyHash via sha256Hex at line 75 inline, then inserts. api-key.ts:5-11 has identical private hashKey; validateApiKey re-derives prefix slice(0,8) at 40, hash at 41, lookup by keyPrefix at 46, timingSafeEqualHex at 48. Digests byte-identical today but structurally uncoupled; a future hashKey change would silently break validation of new keys. The 8-char prefix is a bare slice(0,8) in both files, no shared constant, and is load-bearing per apiKeys_keyPrefix_idx at schema.ts:168. Single producer confirmed: generateApiKey/sha256Hex exist only in keys.ts; setup-local.mjs mints via the HTTP POST route not duplicated crypto. keys.ts has no test. Precedent: invite-tokens.ts co-locates produce and verify-prep in one lib module and its comment says it mirrors lib/api-key.ts, which is now subtly false. Deletion test passes: concentrates two halves of one invariant into the module that owns the agreement contract. Callers: keys POST (mint), validateApiKey on every ingest request (verify). Medium severity, no ADR conflict.</notes>
</invoke>

#84 · Gap · Capability credentials
Token/credential-bytes crypto (sha256 hex, base64url, timing-safe compare, mint) duplicated across 4 files with no shared seam
medium severity effort: small confidence: high missing locality revised by verifier
Files
  • apps/dashboard/src/lib/artifact-tokens.ts:27
  • apps/dashboard/src/lib/artifact-tokens.ts:60
  • apps/dashboard/src/lib/invite-tokens.ts:7
  • apps/dashboard/src/lib/invite-tokens.ts:15
  • apps/dashboard/src/lib/api-key.ts:5
  • apps/dashboard/src/lib/api-key.ts:13
  • apps/dashboard/routes/api/teams/[teamSlug]/p/[projectSlug]/keys.ts:9
  • apps/dashboard/routes/api/teams/[teamSlug]/p/[projectSlug]/keys.ts:17
Problem — the friction

Four primitives that handle credential bytes are re-derived in divergent forms across four files, and three of the four call sites are exactly the security primitives. (1) SHA-256→hex digest appears verbatim three times — api-key.ts hashKey (line 5), invite-tokens.ts hashInviteToken (line 15), keys.ts sha256Hex (line 9) — same .map((b)=>b.toString(16).padStart(2,"0")).join("") idiom. (2) base64url-encode of random/HMAC bytes appears three times — artifact-tokens.ts base64urlEncode (line 27) as a named helper, invite-tokens.ts generateInviteToken (line 7) inline, keys.ts generateApiKey (line 17) inline — same btoa(...).replace(/\+/g,"-").replace(/\//g,"_").replace(/=+$/,"") chain. (3) timing-safe compare exists in two DIVERGENT forms: artifact-tokens.ts timingSafeEqual (line 60) over a Uint8Array vs api-key.ts timingSafeEqualHex (line 13) over a hex string via charCodeAt — same intent, two implementations, one of which compares twice the bytes (hex is 2x). (4) random-token minting (getRandomValues(24) → base64url) is duplicated between generateInviteToken and generateApiKey, differing only by the wrf_ prefix. A maintainer or AI auditing 'is our token handling constant-time and correctly padded?' must open four files, diff four idioms, and reason about each independently. A subtle divergence here — a base64url that mis-pads on decode, a compare that early-returns on first mismatch, a digest that drops a leading zero — is an auth-bypass or a timing leak, and nothing forces the four copies to stay identical.

Solution — the deepened module

Extract one token-crypto module (src/lib/token-crypto.ts) owning the credential-bytes primitives behind a small interface: sha256Hex(input: string): Promise<string> (fixed-width lowercase hex), base64urlEncode(bytes: Uint8Array): string using the loop form (NOT String.fromCharCode spread — the spread form in invite-tokens.ts/keys.ts is the divergence to eliminate, since it is unsafe for large inputs), base64urlDecode(str: string): Uint8Array | null, timingSafeEqualBytes(a, b) plus a timingSafeEqualHex(a, b) that decodes hex to bytes and delegates to the byte version so there is exactly one constant-time loop, and mintToken(byteLen = 24, prefix?: string): string. Consumers: artifact-tokens (HMAC sign/verify, the encode/decode/byte-compare source of truth), invite-tokens (mintToken + sha256Hex), api-key (sha256Hex + timingSafeEqualHex), and the keys.ts route handler (mintToken({prefix:"wrf_"}) + sha256Hex). The module interface carries the invariants — encode/decode round-trip, constant-time over equal-length inputs, fixed-width hex, safe encoding for arbitrary-length byte arrays — that are currently re-asserted or silently assumed in four places. The single base64url round-trip is the highest-value part to unify, since the encode idiom genuinely diverges today.

Benefits — leverage & locality

Leverage: callers stop hand-rolling base64url replace-chains and digest loops; minting an opaque credential or hashing a bearer token becomes one named call with the padding/constant-time guarantees baked in. Locality: the security-critical bytes handling lives in one file — a fix to padding, a switch to a hardened compare, or a digest-width change happens once and propagates to all four capability types instead of needing four synchronized edits that nothing enforces. The divergence between the two timing-safe compares collapses to one loop, removing the class of bug where one path is constant-time and the other is not.

Deletion test

Earning its keep. Delete the proposed module and the complexity REAPPEARS across at least 4 callers: artifact-tokens.ts (base64url encode/decode + byte compare for HMAC verify), invite-tokens.ts (mint + sha256Hex), api-key.ts (sha256Hex + hex compare), and the keys.ts route handler (mint + sha256Hex). That is the current state — the duplication is the cost of not having the seam. This is a concentration of complexity currently smeared across 4 sites, not a pass-through.

Testability impact

Today only artifact-tokens has a test (artifact-tokens.test.ts, 54 lines); invite-tokens, api-key's timingSafeEqualHex/hashKey, and keys.ts's inline crypto are entirely untested — the duplicated copies are the untested copies. A single token-crypto module gives one focused unit-test surface: base64url round-trip and mis-pad rejection, sha256Hex against known vectors and leading-zero inputs, constant-time compare over equal/unequal-length inputs, mint uniqueness/length/prefix. These become testable in isolation without standing up Hono, the D1 batch path, or env mocking, and the verify outcomes that currently require integration-level reasoning (does an invite hash match? does an API-key compare reject a near-miss?) are exercised directly.

Interface sketch
// src/lib/token-crypto.ts export function mintToken(byteLen?: number, prefix?: string): string; // getRandomValues -> base64url, optional prefix export async function sha256Hex(input: string): Promise<string>; // fixed-width lowercase hex export function base64urlEncode(b: Uint8Array): string; export function base64urlDecode(s: string): Uint8Array | null; export function timingSafeEqualBytes(a: Uint8Array, b: Uint8Array): boolean; // constant-time over equal-length export function timingSafeEqualHex(a: string, b: string): boolean; // decode -> timingSafeEqualBytes (one loop)
Verifier notes

Verified all four files line-by-line; every cited line is accurate.

(1) SHA-256 -> hex digest: identical .map((b) => b.toString(16).padStart(2, "0")).join("") idiom in api-key.ts:10 (hashKey), invite-tokens.ts:18-20 (hashInviteToken), keys.ts:12-14 (sha256Hex). grep confirms exactly 3 sites, no others.

(2) base64url encode: artifact-tokens.ts:27-31 (named base64urlEncode, builds binary via for-loop), invite-tokens.ts:9-12 (inline), keys.ts:19-22 (inline). grep confirms 3 sites. NOTE a genuine latent divergence the finder underplays: artifact-tokens uses a for loop to build the string, while invite-tokens and keys use String.fromCharCode(...rand) SPREAD — the spread form is fine for 24 bytes but would stack-overflow on a large array, so the two idioms are NOT interchangeable. base64urlDecode exists only in artifact-tokens.ts:33-44 (single copy, since only HMAC tokens are decoded).

(3) timing-safe compare: two divergent forms confirmed — artifact-tokens.ts:60 timingSafeEqual over Uint8Array vs api-key.ts:13 timingSafeEqualHex over hex string via charCodeAt. Both are individually correct and constant-time over equal-length inputs; the hex variant does loop 2x the bytes (64 chars for SHA-256) but that is harmless. Invite-token accept-path compare is delegated to Drizzle hash-match (WHERE keyHash), not a JS compare, so only 2 JS compare impls exist (matches finder).

(4) mint: getRandomValues(24) -> base64url duplicated in invite-tokens.ts:8-12 and keys.ts:18-23, differing only by the wrf_ prefix. grep confirms exactly 2 getRandomValues sites.

No existing shared module: src/lib/ has only artifact-tokens.ts + invite-tokens.ts; no token-crypto.ts/crypto.ts/hash.ts. The four copies are genuinely uncoordinated.

DELETION TEST: passes. The complexity is smeared across 4 files in the current tree (the four listed callers each re-implement 1-2 of these primitives). A shared token-crypto module concentrates it. This is concentration, not a hypothetical one-adapter seam — 4 real consumers exist today (importers verified: api-auth.ts, test-artifact-actions.ts, download.ts consume api-key/artifact-tokens; invites.ts route consumes invite-tokens; keys.ts is self-contained).

ADR check: no durable decision touches token/credential crypto. No conflict.

SEVERITY adjusted high -> medium. The finder's "auth-bypass / timing-leak" framing is partly speculative: each implementation is currently correct, and the hex-vs-byte timing difference leaks nothing exploitable (fixed-width digest). The demonstrable hazards are (a) the encode-idiom divergence (loop vs spread) which is a real correctness gap, and (b) maintenance drift across 4 copies of security-relevant primitives with nothing forcing them to stay identical. These are small (~5-line) helpers, so the concentration payoff is real but modest. Medium is the honest calibration. Core insight is correct; keeping the finding with a sharpened solution.

Gap · Route-shape coupling · 3 findings

#85 · Gap · Route-shape coupling
The ingest-route shape is duplicated byte-for-byte across the Bearer-gate (02) and throttle-gate (03) with no shared matcher — drift silently ships an authed-but-unthrottled or throttled-but-500ing route
medium severity effort: small confidence: high duplication revised by verifier
Files
  • apps/dashboard/middleware/02.api-auth.ts:28-34
  • apps/dashboard/middleware/03.rate-limit.ts:29-33
  • apps/dashboard/middleware/03.rate-limit.ts:71-80
Problem — the friction

The fact 'these five ingest routes (POST /api/runs, POST /api/runs/:id/results, POST /api/runs/:id/complete, POST /api/artifacts/register, PUT /api/artifacts/:id/upload) must each require a Bearer key AND be throttled per-tenant' is a single invariant, but it is expressed twice. RUN_INGEST_RE (/^\/api\/runs(?:\/|$)/) and ARTIFACT_INGEST_RE (/^\/api\/artifacts\/(?:register|[^/]+\/upload)(?:\/|$)/) appear byte-for-byte in both middleware/02.api-auth.ts:28-30 and middleware/03.rate-limit.ts:31-33. There is no shared module — grep confirms the literals exist only in these two files. The auth-gate (02) decides what must present a key; the throttle-gate (03) re-decides what is keyed by apiKey.id. These are intended to be the identical set (03 even relies on 02 having stashed c.var.apiKey first — see 03.rate-limit.ts:74), yet a maintainer renaming /api/runs to /api/test-runs, or adding POST /api/artifacts/:id/finalize, must remember to edit both regexes in two files. Editing only 02 ships an ingest route that is authed but UNthrottled (no per-tenant budget, unbounded D1 writes). Editing only 03 ships a route that is throttled but where the handler still calls getApiKey(c), which throws (api-auth.ts:37-42) because 02 never stashed the key — a 500 instead of a clean 401. Nothing fails at build or test time; the rate-limit.test.ts only exercises /api/runs/abc/results, so a drifted regex passes CI.

Solution — the deepened module

Extract one tiny module, src/lib/ingest-routes.ts, owning only the genuinely-duplicated pair and exposing a single predicate: isIngestRoute(path: string): boolean (true when path matches RUN_INGEST_RE or ARTIFACT_INGEST_RE). 02.api-auth.ts uses it for the early-return gate; 03.rate-limit.ts uses it for the API_RATE_LIMITER branch. The two regex literals then live in exactly one place and the two middleware import the same predicate, so they cannot drift. Do NOT fold AUTH_RE or ARTIFACT_DOWNLOAD_RE into this module — each has a single consumer in 03, so moving them adds a pass-through, not a concentration. Optionally have the matcher also expose the run-vs-artifact-write distinction if a caller needs it, but the binding-name/limiter-selection concern is the sibling triple-encoding finding's territory and should not be conflated here. To close the CI gap, add a rate-limit.test.ts case that drives an /api/artifacts/<id>/upload path through 03 (the artifact branch is currently untested) — the shared predicate makes that assertion meaningful for 02 as well.

Benefits — leverage & locality

Leverage: both middleware callers stop re-deriving route membership from raw regexes — they call a named predicate and get back the surface (and, optionally, the limiter to use). A new ingest endpoint is added by extending one matcher, and it is automatically both authed and throttled. Locality: the answer to 'which routes are the bearer-authed ingest surface' lives in one file a maintainer or AI opens, instead of being reconstructed by diffing two middleware files and hoping they agree.

Deletion test

Earns its keep. Delete the shared matcher and the regexes must reappear in both middleware/02.api-auth.ts and middleware/03.rate-limit.ts (N=2 callers today, N=3 if the auth-route and download-route predicates are folded in). The duplication is exactly the complexity that is currently smeared across the two files; concentrating it is the win. (Contrast: a wrapper that merely re-exports one regex would be a pass-through — but here there are genuinely two independent consumers that must stay byte-identical, so the seam concentrates real coupling.)

Testability impact

Today the only test (rate-limit.test.ts) checks the allow/block/fail-open behavior of checkRateLimit and one happy path through the middleware; it cannot assert that the auth set and throttle set agree because there is no object representing 'the set'. With a matcher module, a single table-driven unit test can assert: every route path under routes/api/runs and routes/api/artifacts/{register,:id/upload} matches the ingest predicate; /api/artifacts/:id/download does NOT match the ingest predicate (it is download-only); and the auth-gated set equals the API_RATE_LIMITER-throttled set. That cross-gate consistency is currently untestable as a unit.

Interface sketch
// src/lib/ingest-routes.ts export type IngestSurface = 'run' | 'artifact-write'; export function matchIngestRoute(path: string): { surface: IngestSurface } | null; // invariant: the bearer-auth gate and the API_RATE_LIMITER throttle gate are BOTH driven by this single predicate.
Verifier notes

Verified all cited code. RUN_INGEST_RE (/^\/api\/runs(?:\/|$)/) and ARTIFACT_INGEST_RE (/^\/api\/artifacts\/(?:register|[^/]+\/upload)(?:\/|$)/) are byte-identical in middleware/02.api-auth.ts:28-30 and middleware/03.rate-limit.ts:31-33. Grep confirms these two literals exist nowhere else in apps/dashboard (excluding .void/ and node_modules) — no shared module. The finding's two failure modes are both real: (a) every ingest handler calls getApiKey(c) (routes/api/runs/index.ts:28, runs/[id]/results.ts:18, runs/[id]/complete.ts:25, artifacts/[id]/upload.ts:17), and getApiKey throws at api-auth.ts:37-42 when c.var.apiKey is absent — so throttling a route in 03 without authing it in 02 yields a 500, not a clean 401; (b) authing in 02 without throttling in 03 leaves an unthrottled ingest route with unbounded D1 writes. The cross-file ordering coupling is confirmed: 03.rate-limit.ts:74 reads c.get("apiKey") that 02 stashes. CI gap confirmed: rate-limit.test.ts only exercises /api/runs/abc/results (lines 71, 84) and imports the middleware directly — it never asserts the route set matches 02's, so a drifted ARTIFACT_INGEST_RE on either side passes CI green.

Deletion test PASSES: the matcher concentrates the regex pair currently duplicated across N=2 real, independent consumers (02 and 03) that must stay byte-identical. Not a hypothetical one-adapter seam — both consumers exist today. Deleting the helper forces the literals back into both files. This is genuine coupling, not a pass-through.

Severity adjusted high->medium: the blast radius is internal and the routes are stable (X-Wrightful-Version pins the protocol at v3; renaming /api/runs is not on any near-term path). The drift only bites on a future route-set edit, and rate limiting is already KNOWN-OUTSTANDING (declared-but-not-provisioned), so today the throttle-gate is partly inert anyway. Real and worth fixing, but not high-urgency.

Scope correction (drives the revise): the finder's "parallel matchAuthRoute / matchDownloadRoute" is speculative. AUTH_RE (03:29) and ARTIFACT_DOWNLOAD_RE (03:30) each have exactly ONE consumer (only in 03). Folding them into the shared module would be a pass-through wrapper for non-duplicated regexes — it fails the deletion test (delete it and nothing reappears across multiple files). The earned win is strictly the ingest-route pair shared by 02 and 03. The "return which limiter applies" benefit is legitimate but belongs to the sibling triple-encoding finding, not load-bearing here. No ADR conflict — small internal helper.

#86 · Gap · Route-shape coupling
Rate-limiter binding-to-path-set pairing exists only as adjacency + prose; no test pins the config-vs-code mapping or the strict/loose budget ordering
low severity effort: small confidence: high leaky seam revised by verifier
Files
  • apps/dashboard/wrangler.jsonc:14-38
  • apps/dashboard/src/lib/rate-limit.ts:18-21
  • apps/dashboard/middleware/03.rate-limit.ts:48-79
Problem — the friction

Three rate limiters (AUTH_RATE_LIMITER, API_RATE_LIMITER, ARTIFACT_RATE_LIMITER) each exist as: (1) a wrangler.jsonc ratelimits entry with a namespace_id and a simple { limit, period } budget (1001/20, 1002/120, 1003/300); (2) a hand-maintained string-union type RateLimiterBindingName in rate-limit.ts:18-21; and (3) a string literal passed at the call site in 03.rate-limit.ts (lines 51, 64, 76). Nothing ties a binding name to the path-set it is meant to throttle. The pairing 'AUTH_RE -> AUTH_RATE_LIMITER keyed by IP', 'ingest -> API_RATE_LIMITER keyed by apiKey.id', 'download -> ARTIFACT_RATE_LIMITER keyed by artifactId' lives only as adjacency in the if/else ladder of 03.rate-limit.ts. A maintainer who adds a fourth limiter to wrangler.jsonc, or renames one, must update the union type by hand and find the matching branch by reading prose comments. The budgets (20/120/300) are invisible to the application code entirely — they are a deploy-time concern in a separate fallback file — so a test can never assert 'the auth limiter is the strict one and the artifact limiter is the loose one'. The 'as never' fakeContext in the test (rate-limit.test.ts:57-67) shows how loosely the call sites are typed against the env.

Solution — the deepened module

Drop the LIMITERS-spec-table-as-deepening. The three pairings are three adjacent branches in one function, not complexity smeared across N callers — restructuring them into a table-and-loop in the same file moves code without concentrating leverage, and yields only a one-consumer (hypothetical) seam. Also note the exported rateLimit() per-route factory is currently dead code (no callers) — either wire it or delete it.

Keep only the high-value, low-cost piece: add a vitest that imports the binding-name union/literals used by 03.rate-limit.ts and parses wrangler.jsonc, asserting (a) bijection between binding names referenced in code and ratelimits[].name entries, and (b) the intended budget ordering (AUTH limit < API limit < ARTIFACT limit). This pins the "auth is the strict one, artifact is the loose one" invariant that today exists only as prose, and catches a renamed/added/removed limiter at test time. This does not require duplicating budgets into TS.

Benefits — leverage & locality

Leverage: the middleware stops being three near-identical hand-coded branches (match path -> compute key -> call checkRateLimit -> 429) and becomes one loop over a declarative table; adding a limiter is one table entry. Locality: 'which limiter throttles which path-set, keyed by what' is one table a maintainer reads top-to-bottom, instead of correlating a jsonc file, a union type, and an if-ladder. The binding-name string stops being free-floating — it is owned by the spec and flows to the checkRateLimit call.

Deletion test

Earns its keep (concentrates). Delete the spec table and the path-set->binding->key triples reappear smeared across the three branches of 03.rate-limit.ts plus the hand-maintained union in rate-limit.ts. N callers: the 3 throttle branches + the union type declaration. The wrangler.jsonc entries themselves cannot be deleted (Cloudflare needs them), so the deploy-config stays — but the application-side re-encoding of names is exactly what the table concentrates.

Testability impact

Makes two things testable that are not today: (a) a table-driven test that for each LIMITER entry, a representative path matches its predicate and routes to the right binding with the right key strategy (IP vs apiKey.id vs artifactId) — currently you can only test one limiter via the 'as never' fakeContext; (b) a config-consistency test that the binding names referenced in code exactly match the ratelimits block in wrangler.jsonc, catching a typo'd or removed limiter before deploy rather than at runtime fail-open (which silently disables throttling — the original MVP bug this whole subsystem was scarred by).

Interface sketch
const LIMITERS = { auth: { binding: 'AUTH_RATE_LIMITER', match: matchAuthRoute, key: byIp }, ingest: { binding: 'API_RATE_LIMITER', match: matchIngestRoute, key: byApiKeyOrIp }, download: { binding: 'ARTIFACT_RATE_LIMITER', match: matchDownloadRoute, key: byArtifactId }, } as const; type RateLimiterBindingName = (typeof LIMITERS)[keyof typeof LIMITERS]['binding'];
Verifier notes

Friction is real but smaller than framed, and the deletion test fails.

Verified facts:
- wrangler.jsonc:14-38 declares three ratelimits entries (AUTH_RATE_LIMITER 20/60, API_RATE_LIMITER 120/60, ARTIFACT_RATE_LIMITER 300/60). The finder wrote periods as "20/120/300" budgets and "1001/20" etc; the limits are 20/120/300 and the periods are all 60. Namespace_ids are 1001/1002/1003. Minor mislabel, but the triple-encoding claim holds.
- rate-limit.ts:18-21 hand-maintains the RateLimiterBindingName string union. Real.
- 03.rate-limit.ts:48-80 has three if-branches pairing path-regex -> binding-name string literal -> key strategy (clientIp / download[1] / apiKey.id). Real adjacency-only coupling. The pairing prose lives in the doc comment lines 9-18.

Where the finding breaks down (deletion test):
- I grepped every importer of @/lib/rate-limit (routes/, middleware/, src/). The ONLY runtime consumer of the binding names + union is middleware/03.rate-limit.ts. The exported rateLimit() per-route factory (rate-limit.ts:50-61) is NEVER called anywhere in the app — it is dead code. The finder did not account for this; it weakens, not strengthens, the case.
- The finder's claimed "N callers = 3 throttle branches + the union declaration" conflates three SIBLING if-branches inside ONE 40-line function in ONE file with N independent callers smeared across the codebase. They are already co-located. Converting them to a LIMITERS table + loop in the same file is a within-module reorganization (a MOVE), not concentration of complexity that currently reappears across multiple callers. Delete the proposed table and the complexity reappears only in the same single function — that is the signature of a pass-through reshuffle, not a deep module.
- The proposed LIMITERS spec would have exactly one consumer (the middleware loop): a hypothetical one-adapter seam, not a real seam earning leverage.
- "Budgets invisible to app code" is true but DELIBERATE per the comments (rate-limit.ts:4-12, wrangler.jsonc:3-11): Void's config schema rejects ratelimits in void.json so they live in the wrangler fallback file, and checkRateLimit fails open in dev. Mirroring budgets into TS as the finder suggests creates a SECOND source of truth that drifts unless guarded by a test.

The one load-bearing, genuinely useful idea: a unit test that reads wrangler.jsonc and asserts (a) every binding name referenced in 03.rate-limit.ts has a matching ratelimits entry and vice-versa, and (b) the budget ordering auth < ingest < artifact. This closes real config-vs-code drift and is the only thing testing the "auth is strict, artifact is loose" intent. But it is a TEST addition, not a deepening, and needs no spec table.

No ADR conflict (the wrangler fallback file pattern is consistent with the single-D1/Void decisions; nothing here proposes reversing a durable decision). checkRateLimit / clientIp are already the deep shared helper (correctly used by both the dead factory and the live middleware), so the deepening that mattered is already done.

#87 · Gap · Route-shape coupling
rateLimit() per-route factory is dead code masquerading as a co-equal throttling seam
low severity effort: small confidence: high dead / vestigial
Files
  • apps/dashboard/src/lib/rate-limit.ts:50-61
  • apps/dashboard/src/lib/rate-limit.ts:27-37
Problem — the friction

rate-limit.ts exports a rateLimit(bindingName, keyFn) factory (lines 50-61) that returns a per-route middleware. A repo-wide grep shows it is imported nowhere — only checkRateLimit and clientIp are consumed (by 03.rate-limit.ts). Worse, the checkRateLimit doc comment (lines 33-37) actively asserts 'Shared by the rateLimit() per-route factory AND the global middleware/03.rate-limit.ts path-matched gate so both honor identical fail-open-in-dev semantics' — describing a two-adapter seam that does not exist. A maintainer or AI reading this believes there are two supported ways to wire throttling (per-route via the factory, or global via the matcher) and may reach for the dead factory, or hesitate to refactor the global middleware for fear of breaking the phantom second consumer. It is a fake seam: the comment claims two adapters, but there is one.

Solution — the deepened module

Delete the rateLimit() factory (rate-limit.ts:50-61) and correct the checkRateLimit doc comment (lines 33-37) to describe its single real caller. Decisive evidence for delete-over-promote: the global middleware doesn't merely skip the factory, it re-implements its own 429 (tooManyRequests with a retry-after header the factory omits), so the factory isn't even a building block of the live path — promoting it would mean rewriting working middleware to adopt a weaker response. Revised comment should read roughly: "Used only by the global middleware/03.rate-limit.ts path-matched gate; fails open in dev so the limiter is inert under miniflare and active on the deployed worker." Leaving the global path-matched middleware as the sole adapter matches the matcher/spec-table direction.

Benefits — leverage & locality

Leverage: removes a false affordance — callers stop seeing a per-route throttling API that nobody maintains or tests. Locality: there is one documented, exercised way to throttle, so a maintainer touching rate limiting does not have to reason about whether the per-route factory must keep working. The doc comment stops lying, which is the highest-leverage fix for AI navigation.

Deletion test

Deleting rateLimit() removes complexity with zero callers re-incurring it (N=0) — the textbook vestigial-module result, so deletion is correct. (This is the inverse of the matcher finding: that one reappears across 2-3 callers when deleted and so must stay; this one reappears nowhere and so must go.)

Testability impact

Neutral-to-positive: the factory is currently untested (only checkRateLimit and the middleware have tests), so deleting it removes an untested public export from the surface. If instead it is promoted to the real seam, it becomes the single unit-testable throttling primitive that route handlers compose, replacing the bespoke if-ladder in the middleware.

Interface sketch
// after: rate-limit.ts exports only checkRateLimit + clientIp; // comment on checkRateLimit reads 'used by middleware/03.rate-limit.ts (the sole throttle adapter)'.
Verifier notes

Verified all claims directly against the files.

(1) The factory exists exactly as cited: apps/dashboard/src/lib/rate-limit.ts:50-61 defines rateLimit(bindingName, keyFn) returning a per-route Hono middleware via defineMiddleware.

(2) It is dead. Three independent checks confirm zero callers: a grep for rateLimit( call sites outside the def file returned nothing; a grep across apps+packages for any reference to rate-limit/rateLimit/checkRateLimit/clientIp shows the ONLY consumers of this module are middleware/03.rate-limit.ts (imports checkRateLimit, clientIp) and src/__tests__/rate-limit.test.ts (imports checkRateLimit + the middleware default) — neither touches the rateLimit factory; and git log -S "import { rateLimit }" shows it was never imported in history. apps/dashboard is a private package (@wrightful/dashboard, "private": true) with no index barrel re-exporting it, so it is not a public API surface either.

(3) The phantom seam is real-and-misleading. rate-limit.ts:34-35 asserts checkRateLimit is "Shared by the rateLimit() per-route factory AND the global middleware/03.rate-limit.ts path-matched gate." But 03.rate-limit.ts:45-83 calls checkRateLimit/clientIp directly and re-implements its OWN 429 response (tooManyRequests, lines 35-43, which even adds a retry-after header the factory's c.json({error}, 429) lacks). So the global path shares NEITHER the factory nor its response shape — the comment describes a two-adapter seam that does not exist. One real adapter (the global matcher), one dead one (the factory).

(4) Deletion test passes cleanly: removing rateLimit() re-incurs complexity in N=0 callers — textbook vestigial. This is the honest inverse of a deepening: nothing is concentrated because nothing depends on it.

(5) No durable-decision conflict — rate limiting is not an ADR-settled axis; deleting an unused factory reverses nothing.

Severity correctly low: it's a dead export + a lying doc comment, not a correctness/security defect. I'd note one amplifier specific to THIS repo: it leans heavily on doc comments as architectural truth (the very same file's neighbor 03.rate-limit.ts:25-27 warns "this must remain wired… can't silently become dead code again"), so a comment that fabricates a second consumer is more dangerous here than average — it could make a maintainer hesitate to refactor the global gate for fear of the phantom factory caller. Still low, but the navigability cost is non-trivial.

Gap · Origin / output safety · 3 findings

#88 · Gap · Origin / output safety
ansiToHtml is the app's only untrusted-text→dangerouslySetInnerHTML seam and is completely untested
high severity effort: small confidence: high untestable
Files
  • apps/dashboard/src/lib/ansi.ts:9
  • apps/dashboard/src/components/test-error-alert.tsx:33
  • apps/dashboard/src/components/test-error-alert.tsx:35
  • apps/dashboard/src/components/test-error-alert.tsx:44
Problem — the friction

ansiToHtml(text) is the single HTML-injection sink in the dashboard: test-error-alert.tsx feeds fully attacker-controlled Playwright error text (errorMessage and errorStack — anyone with a project API key can write these via the ingest pipeline) into dangerouslySetInnerHTML on lines 35-36 and 44-45. The XSS-safety of this path rests on a two-part contract that lives only in comments: (a) Anser.escapeForHtml MUST run before Anser.ansiToHtml so raw & < > are neutralised, and (b) use_classes:true MUST be set so Anser emits class="ansi-red-fg" wrappers rather than style="color:..." (a style attribute is an injection surface; an inline attribute even more so). Nothing pins either half. There is no ansi.test.ts (every other file in this policy — content-types, safe-next-path, artifact-tokens — has a test; this one does not). A future refactor that reorders the two Anser calls, drops escapeForHtml on a 'performance' pass, flips use_classes, or swaps Anser for another lib would silently reopen stored XSS on the dashboard origin, and the only signal is a green build. A maintainer auditing the sink in test-error-alert.tsx has to trust a one-line wrapper and a code comment.

Solution — the deepened module

Add src/__tests__/ansi.test.ts (matching the existing policy-peer test location) that locks the contract on the public interface as-is — keep ansiToHtml(text: string): string as the one-liner over Anser, no signature change. Assertions: (1) <script>, <img onerror=...>, and bare " ' < > & all come out entity-escaped with no executable attribute and no raw tag; (2) a javascript: payload stays inert; (3) a known SGR sequence (e.g. \x1b[31m) produces only class="ansi-*" spans and NEVER a style= or on* attribute (assert the output contains class= and does not match /style=|on\w+=/); (4) the ESC-stripped residue case ([31m as literal text) round-trips without being interpreted. Optionally also assert stripAnsi removes both \x1b[..m and bare [..m forms, but keep stripAnsi clearly labelled as a noise-stripper, not part of the XSS contract (its output is React-auto-escaped). The deepening is purely the executable canary; the interface and implementation stay untouched.

Benefits — leverage & locality

LEVERAGE: callers (test-error-alert today, any future error/log renderer) get a sink they can trust by name without re-reading Anser internals or the biome-ignore justification. LOCALITY: the 'is this output safe to dangerouslySetInnerHTML' decision concentrates in one module with one test file, instead of being smeared between a code comment, a biome-ignore line, and a styles.css class table. A regression that reorders the escape/convert calls or flips use_classes now fails a test instead of shipping stored XSS.

Deletion test

Delete ansi.ts and inline Anser into test-error-alert.tsx: complexity REAPPEARS — both the title sink and the stack sink (2 call sites in one component today, plus stripAnsi's 2 sites in flaky-test-row.tsx) would each have to re-encode the escape-then-convert-with-classes ordering, and the XSS contract would have nowhere to be tested. It earns its keep; the gap is that it currently earns its keep with zero tests. N callers: test-error-alert.tsx (ansiToHtml x2), flaky-test-row.tsx (stripAnsi x2).

Testability impact

Today the XSS defense is only exercisable through a rendered React component in an integration/e2e harness that does not yet exist; the pure string→string contract is trivially unit-testable through the interface. Adversarial inputs (<script>, onerror=, javascript:, mixed ANSI+HTML) become first-class regression tests instead of unverified comments.

Interface sketch
sanitizeAnsiToHtml(text: string): string // invariant: output contains no executable HTML — raw &<> are entity-escaped before colourisation, and only class="ansi-*" wrappers are emitted (never style= or on*=); safe to dangerouslySetInnerHTML.
Verifier notes

Every cited fact checks out against the files. ansi.ts:9-11 is exactly Anser.ansiToHtml(Anser.escapeForHtml(text), { use_classes: true }); the two-part XSS contract (escape-first; use_classes to avoid style=/on*= attributes) lives only in the comment on lines 3-8. test-error-alert.tsx:35-36 and 44-45 are the ONLY two dangerouslySetInnerHTML sinks in the entire src/ tree (grep across src/**/*.{ts,tsx} returns nothing else). The text is genuinely attacker-controlled: errorMessage/errorStack flow through ingest.ts from the ingest pipeline and are validated by schemas.ts truncatedText (lines 57-62), which only does s.slice(0, max) — no HTML escaping at ingest, so the ansiToHtml sink on the dashboard origin is the sole XSS defense, writable by anyone holding a project API key. Test gap confirmed: there is no ansi test anywhere, while the three named policy peers all exist (src/__tests__/content-types.test.ts, safe-next-path.test.ts, artifact-tokens.test.ts). DELETION TEST passes: deleting ansi.ts and inlining Anser would re-smear the escape-then-convert-with-classes ordering across the 2 ansiToHtml call sites and leave the XSS contract with nowhere to be asserted — complexity reappears. This is a deep, tiny-interface sanitizer earning its keep; the deficiency is zero tests pinning the contract. No ADR conflict — purely additive (a test file + keeping the same one-line interface). One inaccuracy to correct in the finder's framing, not its conclusion: stripAnsi (flaky-test-row.tsx:132,135) is NOT part of the XSS contract — its output goes to a React title= attribute and the split-first-line text node, both auto-escaped by React, so it is a noise-stripping helper, not a security sink. Counting its 2 sites toward the security deletion test overstates the blast radius. The load-bearing callers for the XSS contract are the 2 ansiToHtml sites in test-error-alert.tsx only. Anser is pinned at ^2.3.5 in package.json; it was not installed in this workspace so I could not statically confirm the API surface, but escapeForHtml/ansiToHtml/use_classes are Anser's documented public API and the code compiles against it in the existing build.

#89 · Gap · Origin / output safety
script-src 'unsafe-inline' (a nonce-CSP regression from the Void migration) removes CSP as a backstop for the lone ansi XSS sink
medium severity effort: large confidence: high leaky seam revised by verifier
Files
  • apps/dashboard/void.json:19
  • apps/dashboard/src/components/test-error-alert.tsx:35
Problem — the friction

The CSP advertises a strong policy (object-src 'none', frame-ancestors 'none', base-uri 'self', form-action 'self') which reads as the origin-execution backstop. But script-src is 'self' 'unsafe-inline'. The single place attacker text reaches innerHTML is ansiToHtml → dangerouslySetInnerHTML (test-error-alert.tsx:35,44). If the ansi escaping contract ever regresses (see the untested-ansi finding) and an injected fragment like < img src=x onerror=alert(1) > or an inline < script > slips through, 'unsafe-inline' means CSP does NOT block the inline handler/script — the one defense most people assume CSP provides for exactly this sink is off. So the layered story ('escaping AND CSP') is really single-layered for the highest-risk path. Whether 'unsafe-inline' is load-bearing is undocumented: it is plausibly required by the SSR/Inertia-style hydration bootstrap or theme/FOUC inline script (middleware/01.head.ts injects theme handling), but nobody has recorded that, so a hardening attempt can't tell if removing it is safe.

Solution — the deepened module

Two viable paths; (a) is now known to be restoration rather than greenfield:

(a) Make CSP a real script backstop by threading a per-request nonce through Void's head injection — emit script-src 'self' 'nonce-...' and tag the FOUC-killer inline script (middleware/01.head.ts) with that nonce, then drop script-src 'unsafe-inline'. Note: the pre-Void rwsdk stack already ran a nonce-based CSP (setCommonHeaders with CSP nonce, per worklog 2026-05-02) that was lost in the migration — so this is restoring a previously-working seam, not inventing one. Scope this to script-src only; style-src 'unsafe-inline' is harder to drop because Tailwind v4 injects runtime inline styles (per the same worklog), so leave style-src for a later pass.

(b) If a nonce path is deferred, record explicitly next to the CSP in void.json (and in a worklog) that script-src 'unsafe-inline' is intentionally load-bearing for the FOUC-killer theme script and Tailwind runtime styles, and that this means CSP does NOT contain inline-script XSS at the ansiToHtml → dangerouslySetInnerHTML sink — so the ansi sanitizer (test-error-alert.tsx / src/lib/ansi.ts) is the SOLE guarantee for that path. This makes the untested-ansi finding strictly higher severity and removes the reviewer ambiguity at one place.

The deepening is making the CSP-vs-ansi-sink relationship an explicit, recorded decision rather than an accidental migration regression.

Benefits — leverage & locality

LEVERAGE: a security reviewer reading void.json learns whether CSP guards the innerHTML sink or whether the sanitizer is the only line — today they'd wrongly assume defense-in-depth. LOCALITY: the 'why unsafe-inline' decision gets one recorded owner (a nonce mechanism or a documented justification) instead of being an unexplained token in a long header string.

Deletion test

Not a module to delete — this is a latent weakening to resolve. Removing 'unsafe-inline' (if a nonce path is added) concentrates the inline-script policy in the nonce-issuing seam; documenting it concentrates the rationale. Either way the friction (a reviewer cannot tell whether CSP protects the one XSS sink) is removed at exactly one place.

Testability impact

If a nonce path is adopted, a test can assert no inline script lacks a nonce and that script-src no longer carries 'unsafe-inline'; today there is nothing to test because the policy permits everything inline. It also reframes the ansi-sink tests as the sole guarantee, raising their importance.

Interface sketch
// void.json (target): "script-src 'self' 'nonce-{{cspNonce}}'" + middleware mints per-request nonce and threads it through head injection; OR a recorded note: 'unsafe-inline required by Void hydration bootstrap — ansi sanitizer is the XSS guarantee, not CSP'.
Verifier notes

Verified all cited code.

CSP (apps/dashboard/void.json:19) is exactly as quoted: script-src 'self' 'unsafe-inline' alongside strong object-src 'none', frame-ancestors 'none', base-uri 'self', form-action 'self'. The strong directives read as an execution backstop; the script-src 'unsafe-inline' quietly defeats that backstop for inline-script/handler injection.

The ansi sink (test-error-alert.tsx:35,44) is genuinely the ONLY dangerouslySetInnerHTML in the entire dashboard — grep across all .ts/.tsx returns exactly these two hits. So the finder's "the single place attacker text reaches innerHTML" claim is literally true. The escaping correctness is the subject of the separate untested-ansi finding; this finding's logic is the conditional "IF escaping regresses, THEN 'unsafe-inline' means CSP gives no backstop," which is structurally true regardless of anser's current behavior. (anser isn't installed in this workspace so I couldn't empirically run a payload, but that's not load-bearing for this finding.)

'unsafe-inline' IS load-bearing — confirmed two ways:
(1) middleware/01.head.ts:25-33 injects a raw inline FOUC-killer theme script via headDefaults.script[].innerHTML with no nonce — a strict script-src 'self' would block it.
(2) worklog 2026-05-02-rwsdk-best-practices-audit.md:23 explicitly notes style-src 'unsafe-inline' is needed for Tailwind v4 runtime-injected styles.

The finder's "undocumented" claim is precise and correct in the way that matters: the relationship between 'unsafe-inline' and the ansi XSS sink is nowhere recorded. The MVP review (docs/reviews/2026-05-29-mvp-review.md:200-202) treats CSP generically as "the XSS containment layer" — which is exactly the false belief the finding flags. CSP with script-src 'unsafe-inline' does NOT contain the inline-script XSS class at that sink.

New evidence that STRENGTHENS the finding and sharpens the solution: the pre-Void rwsdk stack ran a nonce-based CSP — 2026-05-02-rwsdk-best-practices-audit.md:5 says setCommonHeaders with CSP nonce. The MVP review (line 200) confirms the security headers were "lost in the migration." So path (a) is RESTORATION of a real seam that existed before, not a greenfield hypothetical — which de-risks it materially and means the deletion test isn't a one-adapter-only seam.

Deletion test: passes. Not a module deletion. There is exactly one inline-script source today (the FOUC-killer). A nonce path concentrates "what inline content is trusted to execute" into one nonce-issuing seam (Void head injection), letting script-src 'unsafe-inline' drop — concentrating the inline-script policy that is currently implicit and smeared. Documentation path concentrates the rationale at the CSP declaration. Either way the friction (a reviewer can't tell whether CSP guards the one XSS sink) is removed at exactly one place.

Durable decisions: no conflict. Touches none of the D1/DO, logical-tenancy, Better-Auth-table, nuqs, or ETL decisions. adrConflict correctly (none).

Severity medium is fair: defense-in-depth, not an active leak. The finding's own observation — that this makes the untested-ansi finding strictly higher severity — is arguably its most load-bearing consequence.

#90 · Gap · Origin / output safety
Artifact-serving origin-safety: download handler's attachment-forcing + safeContentType override has no regression test tying it to the allowlist
low severity effort: medium confidence: high missing locality revised by verifier
Files
  • apps/dashboard/void.json:19
  • apps/dashboard/src/lib/content-types.ts:11
  • apps/dashboard/src/lib/ansi.ts:9
  • apps/dashboard/src/lib/safe-next-path.ts:1
  • apps/dashboard/routes/api/artifacts/[id]/download.ts:147
Problem — the friction

Four mechanisms defend the exact same invariant — attacker bytes must not execute/render on the dashboard origin — yet each is a standalone file that re-states the threat in its own comment and shares nothing: the CSP header in void.json, the SAFE_CONTENT_TYPES allowlist (with its deliberate SVG/text-html exclusion) in content-types.ts, the ansi escaping in ansi.ts, and the redirect/header-injection guard in safe-next-path.ts. content-types.ts says 'so a teammate can't be tricked into rendering attacker HTML', schemas.ts:160 says 'could be served as text/html and execute', the download handler says 'never renders as HTML/JS on the dashboard origin' — three copies of one sentence, no canonical owner. There is no test or doc that asserts the policies stay mutually consistent. Concretely: SAFE_CONTENT_TYPES excludes image/svg+xml and text/html, and CSP's img-src does allow data: and blob: — if someone widens the content-type allowlist to add a renderable type (or relaxes Content-Disposition: attachment in download.ts), nothing cross-checks that CSP still forbids it executing. A maintainer changing one leg cannot see the other three.

Solution — the deepened module

Don't build a new "origin-safety module" — the content-type allowlist is already the single deep home (content-types.ts, 3 callers route through it). Instead close the narrow silent-drift gap on the artifact-serving leg specifically: (1) add a regression test that asserts the download handler ALWAYS sets Content-Disposition: attachment and runs safeContentType() on the token-carried content-type, regardless of what the token claims — this ties the allowlist to the actual response-building behaviour that currently has zero test coverage; (2) add a one-line comment on the CSP string in void.json pointing at content-types.ts as the companion artifact-serving defense (and vice-versa).
